1 TITL 1901 England & Wales Census
1 PUBL MyHeritage
1 TEXT <b>What can you find in the  census?</b>
2 CONC Census returns can help you determine who your ancestors were, and can also tell you:
2 CONC - Where your ancestors were living
2 CONC - Who they were living with
2 CONC - What their occupations were
2 CONC - If they had any servants
2 CONC - Who their neighbours were
2 CONC - If they had any brothers and sisters
2 CONC - What their ages were at the time of the census
2 CONC - If they had any disabilities.
2 CONC 
2 CONC As well as giving you the above information, the fact that census returns are taken every ten years also allows you to track the movements of your ancestors through time as they perhaps move house, ge
2 CONC t married, have children or even change occupations.
2 CONC 
2 CONC The fields which have been transcribed for the  census are:
2 CONC - First name
2 CONC - Middle name
2 CONC - Last name
2 CONC - Sex
2 CONC - Birth place
2 CONC - Age
2 CONC - Place of residence
2 CONC - County
2 CONC - Relationship to head of household
2 CONC 
2 CONC <b>Why this collection is so valuable</b>
2 CONC Census records are valuable since they can tell you where a person lived at a certain place and time. Censuses were conducted by the federal government and will offer a variety of information, dependi
2 CONC ng on year. Census records can answer questions like where your ancestors were living at the time the census was taken, who they were living with, what their occupations were, who their neighbors were
2 CONC , if they had any brothers and sisters, what their ages were at the time of the census and if they had any disabilities.
2 CONC 
2 CONC <b>Searching the census</b>
2 CONC The golden rule of family history is to check the original historical record, or 'primary source', wherever possible. We have provided clear images of the original census enumeration books for you to 
2 CONC view once you've found the right family in the indexes. When using census returns you should first search the transcriptions to help locate your ancestor in the census, and then view the original imag
2 CONC es to validate your findings. It will also help you see the household in the context of surrounding households. This is particularly important as transcribing an entire census is a huge and difficult 
2 CONC task, and whilst we have used the expertise of our transcribers and the experience of key representatives from the genealogy community to help us translate the records, it is inevitable that there wil
2 CONC l be some errors.
2 CONC 
2 CONC <b>Next steps</b>
2 CONC With the information you gain from these census records, you will have the information you need to search for vital records in the locality where you found your ancestor. Also, the fact that census re
2 CONC turns are taken every ten years also allows you to track the movements of our ancestors through time as they perhaps move house, get married, have children or even change occupations.
1 _TYPE Collection
1 _MEDI 10156
0 @S500019@ SOUR
1 _UPD 5 JUL 2019 10:34:03 GMT -0500 
1 TITL 1911 England & Wales Census
1 PUBL MyHeritage
1 TEXT <b>What is in the 1911 census?</b>
2 CONC In common with the censuses that preceded it, it recorded the following information:
2 CONC - Where an individual lived
2 CONC - Their age at the time of the census
2 CONC - Who (what relatives) they were living with
2 CONC - Their place of birth
2 CONC - Occupation
2 CONC - Details of any guests on the night of the census
2 CONC - Details of any servants they had
2 CONC 
2 CONC Also, depending on an individual's circumstances, additional information could include:
2 CONC - Whether they were an employee or employer
2 CONC - Precise details of the industry or service they worked in
2 CONC - Details of nationality
2 CONC - Duration of their current marriage
2 CONC - Number of children born to that marriage
2 CONC - Number of children still living, and the number who had died
2 CONC - Details of any illnesses or conditions each family member had, and the date these began
2 CONC 
2 CONC <b>Fertility in marriage and occupational data</b>
2 CONC In response to government concerns the 1911 census also asked additional, more specific questions to each household, about fertility in marriage and occupational data.
2 CONC 
2 CONC <b>The 1911 census and the suffragettes</b>
2 CONC Frustrated with the government's refusal to grant women the vote, a large number of women boycotted the 1911 census by refusing to be counted. There were two forms of protest. In the first, the women 
2 CONC (or their husbands) refused to fill in the form, often recording their protest on the household schedule. In the second, women evaded the census by staying away from their home for the whole night, an
2 CONC d so did not lodge their protest on the household schedule. In both cases, any details relating to individual women in the households will be missing from the census. For the family historian, a refus
2 CONC al to fill in the form (accompanied by a protest statement) at least registers the presence of a woman, or women, in the household. But the women who evaded the count by leaving their home for the nig
2 CONC ht are entirely untraceable via the census. The exact number of women who boycotted the census is not known, though some people have estimated that it may be as many as several thousand.
2 CONC DC Thomson Family History service provided to MyHeritage members by agreement with The National Archives, London.
1 _TYPE Collection
1 _MEDI 10446
0 @S500020@ SOUR
1 _UPD 5 JUL 2019 10:39:19 GMT -0500 
1 TITL 1939 Register of England & Wales
1 PUBL MyHeritage
1 TEXT The 1939 Register was conducted in ways like a census and includes similar information. One important difference for genealogists is the full date of birth is recorded in the 1939 Register for each pe
2 CONC rson whereas the traditional censuses conducted in England and Wales only records each person’s age.
2 CONC  
2 CONC The information in this collection was gathered under the authority of the National Registration Act of 1939 – an emergency measure at the start of the Second World War. 65,000 enumerators delivered
2 CONC  the forms ahead of the official registration day. On Friday, 29 September 1939 (National Registration Day) householders were required to record the requested information on the supplied forms. On the
2 CONC  following Sunday and Monday local enumerators visited every household, checked and gathered the completed forms, and issued identity cards to each of the residents in the household.
2 CONC  
2 CONC Of the 42 million individuals who are recorded in this collection, 8.2 million records remain closed due to privacy protection requirements, and about 700,000 additional records appear without full na
2 CONC mes. Records are closed for those individuals who were born less than 100 years ago unless matched to a registered death record. These closed records will be made public and added to this online colle
2 CONC ction on a yearly basis going forward.
2 CONC  
2 CONC * The 1921 Census has not yet been released and is expected to be available online in 2022. The 1951 Census is closed until 2052.
1 _TYPE Collection
1 _MEDI 10678
0 @S500021@ SOUR
1 _UPD 5 JUL 2019 10:52:21 GMT -0500 
1 TITL 1841 England & Wales Census
1 PUBL MyHeritage
1 TEXT In the 1841 Census a policy of rounding down ages was in place. As such, people aged: 15-19 were recorded as 15, 20-24 were recorded as 20, 25-29 were recorded as 25, and so on.
2 CONC 
2 CONC 1841 was the first time that the head of each household was given a form to fill in on behalf of everyone in the dwelling on a set day. This system still forms the basis of the method used today.
2 CONC 
2 CONC <b>What can you find in the 1841 census?</b>
2 CONC Census returns can help you determine who your ancestors were, and can also tell you:
2 CONC - Where your ancestors were living
2 CONC - Who they were living with
2 CONC - What their occupations were
2 CONC - If they had any servants
2 CONC - Who their neighbors were
2 CONC - If they had any brothers and sisters
2 CONC - What their ages were at the time of the census
2 CONC 
2 CONC The fields which have been transcribed for the 1841 census are:
2 CONC - First name
2 CONC - Last name
2 CONC - Sex
2 CONC - Birth county
2 CONC - Age
2 CONC - Address
2 CONC - City
2 CONC - Parish
2 CONC - County
2 CONC 
2 CONC Relation to head of household was not recorded in this census. However, MyHeritage has developed technology to imply the relations based on surname, gender, age and position in the household. Though n
2 CONC ot perfect, the implied relations are often correct.
2 CONC 
2 CONC <b>Why this collection is so valuable</b>
2 CONC Census records are valuable since they can tell you where a person lived at a certain place and time. Censuses were conducted by the federal government and will offer a variety of information, dependi
2 CONC ng on year. Census records can answer questions like where your ancestors were living at the time the census was taken, who they were living with, what their occupations were, who their neighbors were
2 CONC , if they had any brothers and sisters, what their ages were at the time of the census and if they had any disabilities.
2 CONC 
2 CONC <b>Searching the census</b>
2 CONC The golden rule of family history is to check the original historical record, or 'primary source', wherever possible. We have provided clear images of the original census enumeration books for you to 
2 CONC view once you've found the right family in the indexes. When using census returns you should first search the transcriptions to help locate your ancestor in the census, and then view the original imag
2 CONC es to validate your findings. It will also help you see the household in the context of surrounding households. This is particularly important as transcribing an entire census is a huge and difficult 
2 CONC task, and whilst we have used the expertise of our transcribers and the experience of key representatives from the genealogy community to help us translate the records, it is inevitable that there wil
2 CONC l be some errors.
2 CONC 
2 CONC <b>Next steps</b>
2 CONC With the information you gain from these census records, you will have the information you need to search for vital records in the locality where you found your ancestor. Also, the fact that census re
2 CONC turns are taken every ten years also allows you to track the movements of our ancestors through time as they perhaps move house, get married, have children or even change occupations.

1 TITL 1881 England & Wales Census
1 PUBL MyHeritage
1 TEXT <b>What can you find in the  census?</b>
2 CONC Census returns can help you determine who your ancestors were, and can also tell you:
2 CONC - Where your ancestors were living
2 CONC - Who they were living with
2 CONC - What their occupations were
2 CONC - If they had any servants
2 CONC - Who their neighbours were
2 CONC - If they had any brothers and sisters
2 CONC - What their ages were at the time of the census
2 CONC - If they had any disabilities.
2 CONC 
2 CONC As well as giving you the above information, the fact that census returns are taken every ten years also allows you to track the movements of your ancestors through time as they perhaps move house, ge
2 CONC t married, have children or even change occupations.
2 CONC 
2 CONC The fields which have been transcribed for the  census are:
2 CONC - First name
2 CONC - Middle name
2 CONC - Last name
2 CONC - Sex
2 CONC - Birth place
2 CONC - Age
2 CONC - Place of residence
2 CONC - County
2 CONC - Relationship to head of household
2 CONC 
2 CONC <b>Why this collection is so valuable</b>
2 CONC Census records are valuable since they can tell you where a person lived at a certain place and time. Censuses were conducted by the federal government and will offer a variety of information, dependi
2 CONC ng on year. Census records can answer questions like where your ancestors were living at the time the census was taken, who they were living with, what their occupations were, who their neighbors were
2 CONC , if they had any brothers and sisters, what their ages were at the time of the census and if they had any disabilities.
2 CONC 
2 CONC <b>Searching the census</b>
2 CONC The golden rule of family history is to check the original historical record, or 'primary source', wherever possible. We have provided clear images of the original census enumeration books for you to 
2 CONC view once you've found the right family in the indexes. When using census returns you should first search the transcriptions to help locate your ancestor in the census, and then view the original imag
2 CONC es to validate your findings. It will also help you see the household in the context of surrounding households. This is particularly important as transcribing an entire census is a huge and difficult 
2 CONC task, and whilst we have used the expertise of our transcribers and the experience of key representatives from the genealogy community to help us translate the records, it is inevitable that there wil
2 CONC l be some errors.
2 CONC 
2 CONC <b>Next steps</b>
2 CONC With the information you gain from these census records, you will have the information you need to search for vital records in the locality where you found your ancestor. Also, the fact that census re
2 CONC turns are taken every ten years also allows you to track the movements of our ancestors through time as they perhaps move house, get married, have children or even change occupations.
